Al-Saybi's Poem: Conditions for Being upon the Truth
Poem enumerating four conditions for certain truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conditions that al-Saybi's poem identifies for being upon certain truth in theology, jurisprudence, and Arabic grammar.

Core Idea
Truth requires agreement with al-Ash'ari, sincerity with al-Shafi'i, and specific grammar views.

This lesson presents a concise poem recorded by Ibn Asakir. The poem, composed by Judge al-Saybi, outlines a set of criteria for attaining certainty in religious knowledge. It combines theology, law, and language as pillars of orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and its chain
First, read the Arabic poem and its translation. Note the chain of transmission that Ibn Asakir provides, which shows the poem's authority.
The chain includes several notable scholars, but the focus is on the poem's content.
2
Identify the four conditions
The poem lists four conditions. Find each one in the text and understand what each requires.
The conditions involve theology, jurisprudence, and grammar.
3
Reflect on the choice of scholars
Consider why al-Ash'ari and al-Shafi'i are chosen for theology and law, and why Ibn al-'Ala' is praised in grammar while al-Mubarrad is warned against.
This reveals a specific scholarly lineage.
4
Understand the conclusion
The poem concludes that fulfilling these conditions places one on the certain truth. Reflect on how these fields together define orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدنى الشيخ الزاهد أبو محمد عبدالوارث بن عبدالغني الأصولي لبعضهم وكتب إلى الشيخ أبو القسم نصر بن نصر العكبري يخبرني عن القاضي أبى المعالى عزيزى بن عبد الملك قال أنشدنا القاضي الإمام أبو الحسن هبة الله بن عبد الله السيبي مدرس وملقن ولى العهد في العالمين أبى عبدالله بن محمد بن الامام امير المؤمنين القائم بأمر الله عبد الله أبى القسم عبد الله -جعفر إذا كنت في علم الأصول موافقا بعقدك قول الأشعرى المسدد وعاملت مولاك الكريم مخالصا بقول الامام الشافعي المؤيد وأتقنت حرف ابن العلاء مجردا ولم تعد في الإعراب رأى المبرد فأنت على الحق اليقين موافق شريعة خير المرسلين محمد
The ascetic shaykh Abu Muhammad Abd al-Warith ibn Abd al-Ghani al-Usuli recited to me for someone, and wrote to shaykh Abu al-Qasim Nasr ibn Nasr al-Ukbari informing me from the judge Abu al-Ma'ali Azizi ibn Abd al-Malik, he said: The judge imam Abu al-Hasan Hibatullah ibn Abdullah al-Saybi, teacher and instructor of the heir to the world, Abu Abdillah ibn Muhammad ibn al-Imam Amir al-Mu'minin al-Qa'im bi-Amr Allah Abd Allah Abu al-Qasim Abd Allah – Ja'far, recited to us: If you are, in the science of principles, in agreement with your contract with the words of al-Ash'ari the rightly guided, and you treat your generous Lord sincerely with the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i the supported, and you master the letters of Ibn al-'Ala' purely, and you do not go beyond the opinion of al-Mubarrad in syntax, then you are upon the certain truth, in agreement with the Shari'a of the best of messengers, Muhammad.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a long chain of transmission leading to a poem by Judge al-Saybi. The poem lists four conditions for being upon certain truth: (1) agreeing with al-Ash'ari in the science of principles (usul al-din), (2) sincerely following Imam al-Shafi'i in jurisprudence, (3) mastering the grammatical approach of Ibn al-'Ala' (a famous grammarian) without deviation, and (4) not adopting the syntactic views of al-Mubarrad. The poem concludes that fulfilling these makes one adhere to the Shari'a of Prophet Muhammad. It emphasizes that correct theology (Ash'ari), correct fiqh (Shafi'i), and correct grammar (Ibn al-'Ala' rather than al-Mubarrad) are all necessary for truth.

Key Terms
علم الأصول
قول الأشعرى
الامام الشافعي
ابن العلاء
المبرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem is sometimes misread as opposing all grammar schools, but it specifically praises Ibn al-'Ala' and criticizes al-Mubarrad.
Correction: The poem does not reject grammar or scholarship; it promotes a specific grammatical tradition (Ibn al-'Ala') while warning against another (al-Mubarrad).

Checkpoint

A) Al-Ash'ari, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn al-'Ala'
B) Al-Ash'ari, Abu Hanifa, and al-Mubarrad
C) Al-Ash'ari, Malik, and Sibawayh
D) Al-Ash'ari, al-Shafi'i, and al-Mubarrad

Key Takeaway
The poem asserts that certain truth requires following al-Ash'ari in theology, al-Shafi'i in jurisprudence, and a specific grammatical tradition represented by Ibn al-'Ala', while avoiding the grammatical views of al-Mubarrad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The poem says you must be 'in agreement with your contract' with al-Ash'ari. Is your adherence to the Ash'ari school based on personal conviction and study, or is it just what you were raised with? How can you make it more sincere?
?
Are there any teachings or influences you follow that might contradict the condition of avoiding al-Mubarrad's opinions? How can you ensure you are not straying from the path of certain truth as defined here?

The Warning Against Whims and the Call to Ash'ari Creed
Ibn Asakir records a poem criticizing whims and urging Ash'ari creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this poem uses stark imagery to condemn following whims and to affirm the Ash'ari creed as the path of truth.

Core Idea
Whim blinds; truth is in the creed of al-Ash'ari.

In his biographical work, Ibn Asakir quotes a poem that sharply criticizes the state of his contemporaries, who have abandoned true guidance for blind desires. The poet then issues a clear command to cling to the truth embodied in the Ash'ari school. This passage illustrates the use of poetry as a vehicle for creedal affirmation and moral admonition.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the stark contrast between the people's state and the command at the end.
The poem relies on strong visual imagery like 'blind confusion' and 'whim does not see'.
2
Identify the two parts of the poem
The poem first describes the negative situation (people in blind confusion, making their religion whim), then gives a positive command (adhere to truth, believe in Ash'ari creed). Notice how these two parts relate.
This structure – condemnation then exhortation – is common in admonitory poetry.
3
Reflect on the role of poetry in creed
Ask yourself why Ibn Asakir would include a poem in a historical/biographical work. What does this tell you about the transmission of creed?
Poems were memorized easily and served as concise summaries of belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدني الشيخ ابو الفتح ناصر بن عبد الرحمن القرشي لبعضهم : أصبح الناس في عمى بين جعلوا دينهم هوى والهوى غير مبصـ وتعاموا عن الهدى ليس فيهم بمنكر وهو من جهلهم برى فالزم الحق لا تزغ واعتقد عقد الأشعرى
Shaykh Abu al-Fath Nasir ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Qurashi recited to me for someone: People have become in blind confusion; they made their religion whim, and whim does not see. They turned away from guidance, there is none among them to disapprove, and he is free from their ignorance. So adhere to the truth, do not deviate, and believe in the creed of al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a poem citation. The poet describes people as being in a state of blindness (عمى) and confusion, having taken their desires (هوى) as their religion, which is blind. They have turned away from guidance, and there is no one to rebuke them – the poet distances himself from their ignorance. The poem then commands the reader to cling to the truth, not to swerve, and to adopt the creed (عقد) of al-Ash'ari. It thus presents the Ash'ari school as the embodiment of truth in a time of widespread error.

Key Terms
عمى
هوى
عقد الأشعرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To follow the majority
B) To adhere to the truth and believe in the Ash'ari creed

Key Takeaway
The poem starkly contrasts the blindness of following whim with the clear truth of the Ash'ari creed, making adherence to it a religious duty.

Al-Jazari's poem defending Ash'ari theology
Poem defending Ash'ari theology, emphasizing divine speech is unlike created speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jazari uses poetry to defend Ash'ari theology, especially the doctrine that God's speech is unlike created speech.

Core Idea
Poetry as a weapon: defending the uniqueness of God's speech against accusations of anthropomorphism.

This lesson presents a poem by Abu al-Qasim al-Jazari, preserved by Ibn Asakir, which defends the Ash'ari position on divine attributes, especially speech. The poet laments the proliferation of innovation and praises Imam al-Ash'ari as the renewer of guidance. He then directly refutes the claim that Ash'aris equate God's speech with human speech, calling such a charge an abomination.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem
Read the Arabic poem and its translation, noting the flow of argument.
The poem opens with general statements about innovation.
2
Identify the complaint
Notice the opening focus on innovations and division in the community. The poet warns of Muslims splitting after unity.
3
Praise of al-Ash'ari
See how al-Ash'ari is presented as a divinely aided imam who benefits creation. The poet highlights his piety and role in spreading guidance.
4
Refutation of the accusation
Focus on the lines denying that God's speech is like heard speech. The poet declares that non-Ash'aris are ignorant.
5
Disassociation
Understand the poet's strong disavowal of those who claim God's speech is like created speech, calling it an ugly abomination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدني ابو محمد عبد الله بن محمد الاسكندراني لأبي القسم الجزري الاسكندراني : خذ ما بدا لك أو فدع كثرت مقالات البدع ان النبي المصطفى دينا حنيفا فيا شرع ورضي به عيـاده رب تعالى فارتفع قد كان دينا واحدا حتى تفرق ما اجتمع قوم اضلهم الهـوى والآخرون لهـ سم تبع الله اید شیـ الأشعرى إمام خنا وبه البرية قد نفع يخ الديانة والورع بسط المقالة بالهدى وفظيع حجتهم قطع تى استضيء بنوره والله يتقن ما صنـ من قال غـ سير مقاله أخطا الطريقه وابتدع لا ينكرن كـــلامـه الا اخو جهل لكع اهل العقول تيقظوا فالفجر في الافق انصدع نسبوا الى رب العلى ما قوله منه زعموا بأن كلامـه مثل الكلام المستمع فبرئت منهم انهـ ركبوا قبيحات الشنع
Abu Muhammad Abd Allah ibn Muhammad al-Iskandarani recited to me for Abu al-Qasim al-Jazari al-Iskandarani: Take what appears to you or leave; the articles of innovation have multiplied. Indeed the chosen Prophet (brought) a pure religion, so woe to those who [oppose] law. And his servants are pleased with him; Lord, exalted, high. It was a single religion until it split, what was united. A people led astray by desire, and others following their own whims. Allah aided shaykh al-Ash'ari, the imam of [knowledge], and through him the creation benefited. [He is] the one of piety and abstinence. He spread the discourse with guidance and cut their terrible arguments. So that one may seek light from his light, and Allah perfects what He does. Whoever says other than his article has missed the path and innovated. None denies his speech except a brother of ignorance, a fool. People of intellect, be awake, for dawn has split the horizon. They attributed to the Lord of the Most High what He did not say; they claimed that His speech is like heard speech. So I disassociate from them, for they have mounted ugly abominations.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy poem in defense of al-Ash'ari. The poet begins by acknowledging the proliferation of innovations (بدع) and declares the Prophet's religion was originally one until divisions occurred, led by desire. He then praises al-Ash'ari as an imam through whom Allah benefited humanity, spread guidance, and refuted opponents' arguments. The poet warns that those who reject al-Ash'ari's speech are ignorant. He calls people of intellect to awaken, as the dawn has broken (truth has become clear). The poem then addresses the accusation that Ash'aris attribute to God what He did not say, specifically the claim that God's speech is like created speech. The poet disassociates from such accusation, stating that the accusers have committed ugly abominations. Thus the poem defends Ash'ari theology on divine speech.

Key Terms
بدع
الأشعرى
كلام الله
المستمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe that God's speech is like human speech (created, similar).
Correction: The poem explicitly rejects this: 'They claimed that His speech is like heard speech. So I disassociate from them.' Ash'aris affirm God's speech as an eternal attribute, not comparable to created speech.

Checkpoint
What accusation against the Ash'aris does the poet reject?
A) That they deny God's attributes
B) That they believe God's speech is like heard speech
C) That they follow desire
D) That they innovate in religion

Key Takeaway
Al-Jazari's poem defends the Ash'ari doctrine of God's speech as uncreated and not comparable to human speech, and he dissociates from those who claim otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an accusation against a group of Muslims, do you rush to accept it, or do you investigate the truth like the poet did? Take one specific accusation you have heard recently and commit to finding out the actual belief from reliable sources.
?
The poem says 'people of intellect, be awake, for dawn has split the horizon.' Are you intellectually awake in your faith, or are you slumbering through opinions and desires? What is one area where you have been following your own whims rather than seeking the truth?
?
Have you ever attributed to God something He did not say, perhaps through faulty reasoning or by comparing Him to creation? Examine your own understanding of God's attributes: do you truly affirm them without likeness, or is there a subtle anthropomorphism in your heart?

A Poetic Rebuke: Defending Ash'ari Transcendence
Ibn Asakir records a poem rebuking opponents of Ash'ari theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this anonymous poem defends Ash'ari theology by rebuking opponents who promote innovation, partisanship, and anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The poem rebukes opponents who plant anthropomorphism in barren hearts.

Ibn Asakir closes a chapter with an anonymous poem that directly addresses those who oppose Ash'ari teachings. The poet commands the opponent to stop spreading innovations and to abandon partisanship. He accuses them of being slaves to greed and of futilely trying to promote anthropomorphism (tashbih) while the truth of divine transcendence (tanzih) has already dawned.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem once through, then read its literal translation. Let the poet's rhythm and tone come through before analyzing.
The poem uses strong rebuking language.
2
Identify the accusations
List the charges the poet makes against the opponent: innovation, partisanship, cursing scholars, ignorance, greed, and planting anthropomorphism.
Notice the contrast between tashbih (anthropomorphism) and tanzih (transcendence).
3
Reflect on the defense of tanzih
Consider how the poem defends the Ash'ari commitment to divine transcendence by accusing opponents of failed anthropomorphism.
The poem implies that Ash'aris uphold tanzih correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنشدني بعض اصحابنا لبعض اهل العصر في وزن هذه الأبيات : قل للمخالف يا لكع كف اللسان عن البدع وذر التعـصب جانبا واللعن للعلماء دع فظلام جهلك في العقيدة قد تلاشى وانقشع ممن ينزه وانصدع قيته ماء الخداع قد انقطع لما بدا فـجر الهدى ما انـت حلف زهادة بل انت عبد للطمع كم تزرع التشبيه في سبخ القلوب فما انزرع
And some of our companions recited to me for someone of the time in the meter of these verses: Say to the opponent, O you fool: restrain your tongue from innovation. Leave aside partisanship, and stop cursing the scholars. The darkness of your ignorance in creed has faded and dispersed. From the one who declares [God's] transcendence, his [؟] has broken, the water of deceit has ceased. When the dawn of guidance appeared, you are no ally of asceticism; rather you are a slave of greed. How much you plant anthropomorphism in the salty soil of hearts, and it does not grow.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a final poem from the chapter. It is addressed to an opponent (المخالف) who is called a fool (يا لكع). The poet commands him to stop speaking of innovations, abandon partisanship, and cease cursing scholars. The poet claims that the opponent's ignorance has dissipated. The opponent is accused of being a slave of greed, not a true ascetic. The poem criticizes the opponent for trying to plant anthropomorphism (تشبيه) in barren hearts, but it fails. Thus the poem defends the Ash'ari commitment to transcendence (تنزيه) and rejects anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
مخالف
بدع
تعصب
تشبيه
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris reject divine attributes entirely.
Correction: The poem defends tanzih (transcendence) but does not deny attributes; it opposes anthropomorphism (tashbih).

Checkpoint

A) Innovation
B) Anthropomorphism
C) Heresy
D) Partisanship

Key Takeaway
This poem defends Ash'ari theology by rebuking opponents who promote anthropomorphism and innovation, affirming divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have I ever dismissed or cursed a scholar simply because their views differed from my school? What does this poem reveal about the arrogance of such an attitude, and will I repent from it today?
?
The poet accuses the opponent of being 'a slave of greed.' Where in my own heart do I cling to partisan or worldly attachments that prevent me from accepting the truth when it becomes clear?
?
When the 'dawn of guidance' appears to me through clear evidence, am I willing to let go of my ignorance and stubbornness, or do I continue planting falsehood in my heart out of pride?

Rebuking the Opponent's Anthropomorphism
Rebuke and accusation against anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's accusation against opponents who spread anthropomorphism (tashbih) and his call to refrain from innovation and slander.

Core Idea
Opponents sow anthropomorphism in barren hearts, but it cannot take root.

The poem opens with a sharp rebuke to an unnamed opponent, accusing him of spreading anthropomorphism and ignorance. The poet commands him to restrain his tongue from innovation and to stop cursing scholars. This sets the stage for praising al-Ash'ari's correct methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening rebuke
Read verses 1–4 where the poet addresses the opponent as 'vile' and tells him to stop slandering scholars and spreading innovation.
Notice the harsh tone: 'Say to the opponent, O vile one, restrain your tongue from innovation.'
2
Identify the accusation
Focus on the line 'How often you sow anthropomorphism in salty hearts, but it does not take root.' This is the core accusation.
The accusation is specifically spreading tashbih (anthropomorphism).
3
Understand the context
The poet advises the opponent to leave Damascus because his falsehood cannot find acceptance among its people.
Damascus was a center of Ash'ari scholarship, so the opponent's views would be rejected there.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنشدني بعض اصحابنا لبعض اهل العصر في وزن هذه الأبيات :
قل للمخــــــــــــالف يا لكع كف اللسان عن البدع
وذر التعـ[؟] صب جانبا واللعن للعلماء دع
فظلام جهلك في العقيدة قد تلاشى وانقشع
ممن ينزه[؟] وانصدع
قيته[؟] ماء الخداع قد انقطع
لما بدا فـ[؟] وغراس ما أسـ[؟] جر الهدى
ما انـ[؟] ت حلف زهادة بل انت عبد للطمع
كم تزرع التشبيه في سبخ القلوب فما انزرع
فاهجر دمشق وأهلها واسكن بصرى أو زرع
هناك يمكن ان يص دق ما تقول ويستمع
واعلم بأن الأشعري عدو اصحاب البدع
فهو المجيد الذب عن سنن الرسول وما شرع
ر تقی عالم جمع الديانة والورع
رفع الآلــه مـــــــــحله عند البرية فارتفع
واختار ما قال الرسول من الأصول وما اخترع
لكنه نصب الدليل لمن تسنن واتبع
وأبان أن العقل لا ينفى الصواب المتبع
كان الرسول بها صدع[؟]
یا حسن ما ابدى لنا وجه الدليل وما انتزع
فغدا به شمل الهدى للمسلمين قد اجتمع
وتفرقت فرق الضلال وذل مذموم الشيع
وتعطلت ممن يعطل بعد كثرتهم بقع[؟]
قصد الجدال فما قمع[؟]
فلأي حزب منهم امه[؟] ذو بدعة لحجاجه الا انقطع
لو لم يصنف عمره غيـ[؟] الابانة واللمع لكفى
فكيف وقد تفتـ[؟] ن في العلوم بما جمع
جموعة تربى على الـ[؟] مائتين مما قد صنع
لم يأل في تصنيفها اخذا بأحسن ما استمع
فهدى بها المسترشدين ومن تصفحها انتفع
تتلى معانی کتبه فوق المنابر في الجمع
ويخاف من افــحــامــه أهل الكنائس والبيع
فهو الشجا في حلق من ترك المحجة وابتدع
فعليه رحمة ربه ما غاب نجم أو طلع
And some of our companions recited to me for a contemporary in the meter of these verses:
Say to the opponent, O vile one, restrain your tongue from innovation.
Leave aside fanaticism, and leave cursing the scholars.
For the darkness of your ignorance in creed has dissipated and cleared.
From one who is transcendent [؟] and split.
His [؟] water of deceit has been cut off.
When what appeared [؟] and the planting of what [؟] the trees of guidance.
You are not a companion of asceticism; rather, you are a slave of greed.
How often you sow anthropomorphism in salty hearts, but it does not take root.
So leave Damascus and its people, and reside in Busra or Zar'.
There it may be possible for what you say to be true and be heard.
And know that al-Ash'ari is an enemy of the people of innovation.
He is the excellent defender of the traditions of the Messenger and what he legislated.
A pious scholar who combined religion and scrupulousness.
God raised his station among creation, so it rose high.
He chose what the Messenger said of principles, not what he invented.
But he set up evidence for those who follow the Sunnah and follow.
He made clear that reason does not negate correct consensus?
The Messenger [؟] with it.
How beautiful what he showed us of the face of evidence and what he extracted.
So through him the inclusive guidance of Muslims gathered.
The sects of misguidance dispersed, and blameworthy factions were humiliated.
And those who negate [attributes] were rendered idle after their many spots [؟].
He intended debate, but it was not suppressed [؟].
For any party among them whose mother [؟] an innovator, his argument was cut off.
If he had not authored in his lifetime except al-Ibana and al-Luma', it would suffice.
How then, when he has opened doors in sciences with what he collected.
A collection exceeding two hundred works that he authored.
He spared no effort in their compilation, taking the best what he heard.
So he guided the seekers of guidance through them, and whoever perused them benefited.
The meanings of his books are recited from pulpits in gatherings.
And the people of churches and synagogues fear being silenced by him.
He is a thorn in the throat of whoever abandons the path and innovates.
Upon him be the mercy of his Lord as long as a star appears or rises.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a second poem, also polemical and panegyric. The poet addresses an opponent, telling him to stop slandering scholars. He accuses the opponent of spreading anthropomorphism (tashbih) and advises him to leave Damascus. The core praise of al-Ash'ari follows: he is an enemy of innovators, defender of the Sunnah, and a pious scholar. The poet commends al-Ash'ari for adopting principles from the Prophet, not inventing them, and for using evidence (dalil) for those who follow the Sunnah. He clarifies that al-Ash'ari showed that reason does not negate correct transmitted truth. The poem then highlights al-Ash'ari's voluminous writings, mentioning by title 'al-Ibana' and 'al-Luma'', and states that his collection exceeds two hundred works. These books guide seekers and are even recited from pulpits. Opponents, including people of other faiths, fear him. The poem ends with a prayer for mercy upon al-Ash'ari.

Key Terms
البدع
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the opponent is being accused of disbelief or atheism.
Correction: The poem specifically accuses the opponent of spreading anthropomorphism (tashbih), a theological error, not outright disbelief.

Checkpoint
What accusation does the poet make against the opponent in this poem?
A) That he denies existence of God
B) That he spreads anthropomorphism (tashbih)
C) That he is a companion of asceticism
D) That he authored many books

Key Takeaway
The poet condemns the spread of anthropomorphism and insists that such falsehood cannot gain acceptance among the righteous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone speak about Allah in a way that resembles creation, do you remain silent out of politeness, or do you speak the truth as the poet did? What fear holds you back?
?
The poet accuses the opponent of being a 'slave of greed.' In what ways do your desires for worldly gain influence your beliefs or your treatment of scholars?
?
Are there 'salty hearts' (unreceptive hearts) in your own life—people or situations where you have given up on inviting to truth? Where has Allah placed you as a sower of tawhid?

Praise for al-Ash'ari's Defense of Sunnah
Praise of al-Ash'ari as defender of Sunnah and user of evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's praise for al-Ash'ari as a defender of the Sunnah who used evidence and clarified the role of reason.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari is the enemy of innovators and defends the Prophet's traditions with clear evidence.

After rebuking the opponent, the poet turns to praising al-Ash'ari, describing him as the enemy of innovators and the champion of the Prophet's Sunnah. The poet highlights that al-Ash'ari used evidence (dalil) and made clear that reason does not negate correct transmitted truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the praise of al-Ash'ari
Read verses beginning with 'واعلم بأن الأشعري عدو اصحاب البدع' to 'وأبان أن العقل لا ينفى الصواب المتبع'.
Notice the strong contrast: al-Ash'ari is the enemy of innovators, and the opponent is rebuked.
2
Identify his role
The poet calls al-Ash'ari 'the excellent defender of the traditions of the Messenger' and says he combined religion and scrupulousness.
This is a comprehensive praise of his character and methodology.
3
Understand the use of evidence
The poet emphasizes that al-Ash'ari set up evidence (dalil) for those who follow the Sunnah and made clear that reason does not negate correct transmitted truth.
Ali's popularity is tied to his method of using rational evidence to support orthodox beliefs.

?

Al-Ash'ari's Prolific Works and Impact
Mention of al-Ash'ari's works and their impact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's emphasis on al-Ash'ari's vast scholarly output, especially al-Ibana and al-Luma', and his influence on opposing factions.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari authored over 200 works, including al-Ibana and al-Luma', which guided seekers and silenced opponents.

The final part of the poem extols al-Ash'ari's extensive writings. The poet mentions by name two famous works, al-Ibana and al-Luma', and claims his total authored works exceed two hundred. These books are recited from pulpits and strike fear into the hearts of opponents, including Christians and Jews.

Guided Path
1
Read about his works
Read verses starting from 'يا حسن ما ابدى لنا وجه الدليل وما انتزع' to 'فعليه رحمة ربه ما غاب نجم أو طلع'.
Pay attention to the specific mention of 'al-Ibana' and 'al-Luma'.
2
Note the quantity
The poet states that his works exceed two hundred (تجاوز المائتين). This emphasizes his scholarly dedication.
The number is symbolic of vast productivity.
3
Consider the impact
The poet describes that his books guide seekers, are recited from pulpits, and make opponents (people of churches and synagogues) fear him. This shows the reach of his influence.
The fear of opponents indicates the strength of his arguments.

The poem says al-Ash'ari 'took the best of what he heard' in his compilations. Are you humble enough to learn from others and compile truth, or do you cling to your own opinions? What 'best' have you absorbed lately that could benefit others?
?
People feared being silenced by al-Ash'ari. Do you live in such a way that your commitment to truth makes falsehood uncomfortable, or do you blend in to avoid conflict? What would need to change in your daily life for you to become that 'thorn'?

Classification of al-Ash'ari's Followers into Five Classes
Ibn 'Asakir divides al-Ash'ari's followers into five classes and defines the first class.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn 'Asakir's classification of al-Ash'ari's followers into five classes and the principle that the follower's virtue reflects the followed's virtue.

Core Idea
The virtue of the follower reflects the virtue of the followed.

Ibn 'Asakir introduces his biographical section on the companions of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari by explaining his method. He divides them into five classes, establishing that the excellence of the students testifies to the excellence of the teacher. This lesson presents his classification and the definition of the first class.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the literal meaning. Notice the concise style.
2
Understand the principle
Identify the phrase 'فضل المقتدى يدل على فضل المقتدى به' – the virtue of the follower indicates the virtue of the followed. This is a key methodological statement.
3
Identify the first class
See how Ibn 'Asakir defines the first class: those who directly took from al-Ash'ari or met him and followed his statement or learned from him.
This class includes direct students and contemporaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر جماعة من أعيان مشاهير أصحابه إذ كان فضل المقتدى يدل على فضل المقتدى به وقد قسمتهم خمس طبقات وجدتها على تصحیح قوله متفقات فالطبقة الأولى هم أصحابه الذين أخذوا عنه ومن أدركه ممن قال بقوله أو تعلم منه
Chapter mentioning a group of notable prominent companions [of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari]. Since the virtue of the one followed indicates the virtue of the follower, and I have divided them into five classes, which I found agreeing upon the correctness of his statement. The first class are his companions who took from him and those who encountered him among those who followed his statement or learned from him.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage states the author's methodology for presenting the biographies of al-Ash'ari's followers. Ibn 'Asakir notes that the virtue of the follower (al-muqtada) reflects the virtue of the one followed (al-muqtada bihi). He then announces that he has classified these followers into five categories, and he found these categories consistent in affirming the correctness of al-Ash'ari's school. He then defines the first class: those who were direct students of al-Ash'ari, took knowledge from him, or met him and either followed his doctrine or studied under him.

Key Takeaway
Ibn 'Asakir classifies al-Ash'ari's followers into five classes, with the first class being those who directly learned from or met al-Ash'ari and adhered to his teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the Ash'ari school, but have you personally taken knowledge from a living teacher who embodies that tradition, or are you merely relying on second-hand summaries? What does that say about the depth of your connection to the imam?
?

?
The first class of followers met al-Ash'ari directly. You cannot meet him, but you can learn from those who carry his chain. Are you actively seeking such a chain, or are you content with intellectual assent? What is stopping you from becoming a true follower?

A Student of al-Ash'ari: Abu Abdullah ibn Mujahid al-Basri
Biography of Abu Abdullah ibn Mujahid al-Basri with isnad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Abu Abdullah ibn Mujahid al-Basri as a direct student of al-Ash'ari and teacher of al-Baqillani in the transmission of Ash'ari kalam.

Core Idea
Abu Abdullah ibn Mujahid al-Basri was a key link between al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani.

Ibn 'Asakir transmits a biographical notice about a notable early Ash'ari theologian. This passage shows how the Ash'ari school was transmitted from the founder to the next generation. It also highlights the importance of reliable chains in preserving knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the isnad chain
Notice the chain of narrators: Ibn 'Asakir received this from three teachers, who heard from al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. This shows the care in transmission.
The isnad is a standard feature of Islamic biographical writing.
2
Identify the subject's attributes
Look for descriptions: 'mutakallim', 'companion of al-Ash'ari', from Basra, resided in Baghdad. Note that al-Baqillani studied kalam under him.
3
Recognize the scholarly praise
The passage includes praise for his character and piety, and notes that al-Barqani praised him. This establishes his reliability.
Such praise is typical in biographical entries.
4
Consider the historical significance
Think about how this connects al-Ash'ari to al-Baqillani, a major figure. This demonstrates the early transmission of Ash'ari thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنهم أبو عبدالله بن مجاهد البصري رحمه الله أحمد أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم على بن إبراهيم بن الـعـبـاس الحـسـيـنـي الخطيب وأبو الحسن على بن أحمد بن منصور الغساني الفقيه بدمشق وأبو منصور محمد بن عبدالملك بن خيرون المقرى ببغداد، قالوا أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب الحافظ البغدادی قال : محمد بن ابن محمد ابن يعقوب بن مجاهد أبو عبد الله الطائي المتكلم صاحب أبي الحسن الأشعرى، وهو من أهل البصرة سكن بغداد وعليه درس القاضي أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الكلام وله كتب حسان في الأصول، وذكر لنا غير واحد من شيوخنا عنه انه كان حسن السيرة حسن التدين جميل الطريقة وكان أبو بكر البرقانى يثنى عليه ثناء حسنا وقد ادركه ببغداد فيما احسب ،والله أعلم، أبو بكر البرقانى هو أحمد بن محمد بن أحمد بن غالب الخوارزمي شيخ الخطيب وكان فقيها حافظا متقنا
Among them is Abu Abdullah ibn Mujahid al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him – Ahmad. We were informed by al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim 'Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn al-'Abbas al-Husayni al-Khatib, and Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ghassani al-Faqih in Damascus, and Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Malik ibn Khayrun al-Muqri' in Baghdad; they said: we were informed by Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib al-Hafiz al-Baghdadi; he said: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ya'qub ibn Mujahid Abu Abdullah al-Ta'i al-Mutakallim, the companion of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari – he is from Basra, resided in Baghdad, and under him studied al-Qadi Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-kalam. He has excellent books on usul. More than one of our teachers mentioned about him that he had good conduct, good piety, and a beautiful way. Abu Bakr al-Barqani used to praise him extensively. I think he met him in Baghdad, and Allah knows best. Abu Bakr al-Barqani is Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ghalib al-Khwarazmi, the teacher of al-Khatib; he was a jurist, a hafiz, and precise.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a biographical entry for Abu Abdullah ibn Mujahid al-Basri, a direct student of al-Ash'ari. The author provides an isnad (chain of transmission) from multiple narrators leading to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, who gives the biography. Ibn Mujahid is described as a mutakallim (theologian) from Basra who settled in Baghdad. Notably, the great Ash'ari theologian al-Baqillani (al-Qadi Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib) studied kalam under him. He authored excellent books on usul al-din. The passage further records praise for his character, piety, and method. Additionally, it notes that al-Barqani, a respected jurist and hadith scholar, praised him highly. The author, Ibn 'Asakir, indicates he likely encountered al-Barqani in Baghdad. This biography highlights the scholarly chain from al-Ash'ari through Ibn Mujahid to al-Baqillani, demonstrating the transmission of Ash'ari kalam.

The Intense Devotion of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili
Ibn 'Asakir narrates a story about Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili's intense devotion to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the depth of spiritual absorption that characterized early Ash'ari scholars like Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili.

Core Idea
True devotion to Allah can make one appear distracted to others.

This lesson presents a remarkable anecdote about one of Imam al-Ash'ari's direct students. Through the eyes of the great Qadi al-Baqillani, we see a scholar so absorbed in Allah that he would hide behind a curtain while teaching. This story illustrates the spiritual intensity that was part of the early Ash'ari tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the narrative.
Note the chain of transmission from al-Baqillani through multiple narrators.
2
Identify the key figures
Al-Bahili is the subject, al-Baqillani is the narrator, and al-Isfarayini and Ibn Furak are fellow students. All are major Ash'ari scholars.
3
Understand the hijab (curtain)
Notice that al-Bahili taught from behind a lowered curtain to avoid being seen. This was due to his intense occupation with Allah.
The phrase 'min shiddati ishtighalihi billah' explains his action.
4
Reflect on the description of his state
He is compared to one bewildered or mad (mā'il aw majnūn). This is a spiritual state of complete absorption.
This does not mean literal insanity but a metaphor for overwhelming devotion.
5
Consider the incomplete ending
The text breaks off with 'lam'; speculate what might have followed. The anecdote likely continued with more details.
The incomplete ending reminds us of the fragility of manuscript transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو الحسن الباهلي البصري رحمه الله أخبرني الشيخ أبو المظفر أحمد بن الحسن بن محمد الشعيري ببسطام قال أنا جدى لأمى أبو الفضل محمد بن على بن أحمد السهلكى قال حكى لى واحد من أهل العلم والتصوف عن القاضي أبي بكر بن الباقلاني رحمه الله قال كنت انا والأستاذ أبو اسحق الاسفرايني والأستاذ ابن فورك رحمهما الله معا في درس الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلي، تلميذ الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعرى قال القاضي أبو بكر كان الشيخ الباهلي يدرس لنا في كل جمعة مرة واحدة وكان منا فى حجاب يرخي الستر بيننا وبينه کی لا نراه قال وكان من شدة اشتغاله بالله تعالى مثل واله أو مجنون لـم
And among them is Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him. The Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Sha'iri in Bastam informed me; he said: my maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn 'Ali ibn Ahmad al-Sahlaki narrated to me; he said: one of the people of knowledge and Sufism narrated to me from al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Baqillani, may Allah have mercy on him, who said: I, along with al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and al-Ustadh Ibn Furak, may Allah have mercy on them, were together in the lesson of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, the student of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said: Shaykh al-Bahili would teach us once every Friday, and he was behind a screen; he would lower the curtain between us and him so that we could not see him. He said: due to his intense occupation with Allah Almighty, he was like one bewildered or insane – [incomplete]
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage provides a biographical entry for Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili al-Basri, another direct student of al-Ash'ari. The account is transmitted through a chain from al-Baqillani, who studied under al-Bahili alongside two other major Ash'ari figures: Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Ibn Furak. Al-Baqillani describes that al-Bahili would teach only once a week and would sit behind a curtain (hijab) to avoid being seen, due to his intense devotion to Allah. So absorbed was he in remembrance of Allah that he appeared distracted or even mad. The passage ends incompletely at the page break, but the key point is the extreme spiritual state of al-Bahili, emphasizing his piety and detachment.

Key Takeaway

The Five Classes of al-Ash'ari's Companions
Ibn Asakir introduces the classification of al-Ash'ari's companions into five classes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's classification of al-Ash'ari's companions into five classes and the definition of the first class.

Core Idea
Virtue of students reflects virtue of teacher; al-Ash'ari's companions are grouped into five verified classes.

Ibn Asakir begins this chapter by explaining why he is listing al-Ash'ari's companions: their virtue is a reflection of their teacher's. He then reveals that he has divided them into five classes based on al-Ash'ari's own statement. This lesson introduces that classification and defines the first class.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Ibn Asakir establishes the principle of 'follower's virtue indicates the followed's virtue' before listing the classes.
The passage is a single sentence but contains multiple ideas.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why does Ibn Asakir say 'the virtue of the follower indicates the virtue of the followed'? This justifies why he is listing the companions: it honors al-Ash'ari.
This is a common historical/biographical methodology.
3
Identify the five classes and the first class definition
Ibn Asakir states he divided them into five classes according to al-Ash'ari's own statement. Then he defines the first class: those who directly studied with him, and those who met him and held his view or learned from him.
The remaining classes will be detailed in subsequent sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر جماعة من أعيان مشاهير أصحابه إذ كان فضل المقتدى يدل على فضل المقتدى به وقد قسمتهم خمس طبقات وجدتها على تصحیح قوله متفقات فالطبقة الأولى هم أصحابه الذين أخذوا عنه ومن أدركه ممن قال بقوله أو تعلم منه :
Chapter mentioning a group of the notable prominent ones of his companions, since the virtue of the follower indicates the virtue of the followed. I have divided them into five classes, which I found upon verification of his statement to be in agreement. The first class are his companions who took from him and those who met him from among those who held his opinion or learned from him:
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement by Ibn Asakir for a section listing distinguished companions of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. He explains that the virtue of a follower indicates the virtue of the one followed, so by mentioning these outstanding students, he indirectly honors al-Ash'ari. He states that he has divided these companions into five classes and that he verified that this division aligns with a statement of al-Ash'ari (the phrase 'his statement' likely refers to al-Ash'ari's own classification). He then defines the first class: those who directly studied with al-Ash'ari and those who met him and either held his view or learned from him. This sets the stage for the biographies that follow.

Key Terms
باب
جماعة
أعيان
مشاهير
أصحابه
فضل المقتدى
خمس طبقات
الطبقة الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five classes of al-Ash'ari's companions are based on their geographical origin or date of death.
Correction: Ibn Asakir states that he found the five classes upon verification of al-Ash'ari's own statement, indicating the division is based on the teacher's classification, not external criteria.

C) Verification of al-Ash'ari's own statement
D) Their contributions to writing

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir divides al-Ash'ari's companions into five classes based on al-Ash'ari's own classification, with the first class comprising those who directly studied with him or met him and adopted his views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The virtue of the follower indicates the virtue of the followed. Who are you following in matters of faith? If someone examined your character, would they see the virtue of your teachers reflected in you, or would they see a disconnect? What does your daily conduct reveal about the source of your knowledge?
?
Ibn Asakir considered it important to document the students of al-Ash'ari to honor the teacher. Do you honor your teachers by actually living out their teachings? Or do you take knowledge lightly, without letting it transform your character? Write down one concrete change you will make this week to better reflect the virtue of your teacher.
?

Are you among those who directly take knowledge from the scholar (by reading their works or sitting in lessons), or do you merely hold their opinion without a deep connection? What step can you take today to move closer to the first class?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Veiled Teacher: Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili
Ibn Asakir narrates an anecdote about al-Bahili's spiritual absorption and humility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound spiritual state of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili and how his intense absorption in Allah led him to seclude himself even from his students, and the deep humility among early Ash'ari scholars.

Core Idea
True devotion can make one unaware of the world, even of teaching.

This lesson presents a remarkable anecdote from the early Ash'ari tradition. The great scholar Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a student of al-Ash'ari, was so immersed in contemplation of Allah that he taught from behind a curtain, unaware of his own lesson. Through this story, we glimpse the spiritual intensity and humility that characterized these early masters.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography and its chain of narration
Notice the careful chain from Ibn Asakir back to al-Baqillani. Understand the reliability of the report.
The chain shows the importance of transmission in Islamic scholarship.
2
Visualize the scene: three great scholars attending al-Bahili's lesson
Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, and Ibn Furak were all students of al-Bahili. Reflect on the hierarchy of knowledge.
Even these giants considered themselves students.
3
Reflect on the reason for the veil
Al-Bahili answered that the students see him with the eye of heedlessness. Understand that his state was beyond normal perception.
The veil was not secrecy but protection from distraction.
4
Consider the 'drop in the sea' humility
Each scholar sees himself as a drop compared to his teacher. This shows the tradition of respecting and recognizing the greatness of others.
This humility is a hallmark of true scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو الحسن الباهلي البصري رحمه الله أخبرني الشيخ أبو المظفر أحمد بن الحسن بن محمد الشعيري ببسطام قال أنا جدى لأمى أبو الفضل محمد بن على بن أحمد السهلكى قال حكى لى واحد من أهل العلم والتصوف عن القاضي أبي بكر بن الباقلاني رحمه الله قال كنت انا والأستاذ أبو اسحق الاسفرايني والأستاذ ابن فورك رحمهما الله معا في درس الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلي، تلميذ الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعرى قال القاضي أبو بكر كان الشيخ الباهلي يدرس لنا في كل جمعة مرة واحدة وكان منا فى حجاب يرخي الستر بيننا وبينه کی لا نراه قال وكان من شدة اشتغاله بالله تعالى مثل واله أو مجنون لـم يكن يعرف مبلغ درسنا حتى نذكره ذلك، قال وكنا نسأل عن سبب النقاب وإرسال الحجاب بينه وبين هؤلاء الثلاثة كاحتجابه عن الكل فأجاب إنكم ترون السوقة وهم أهل الغفلة فتروى بالعين التي ترونهم، قال وكانت أيضا جارية تخدمه فكان حالها أيضا كحال غيرها معه من الحجاب وإرخائه الستر، قال أبو المظفر وسمعت جدى يقول سمعت سفيان المتكلم الصوفى رحمه الله يقول سمعت أحمد الفرسانى رحمه الله يقول سمعت الأستاذ أبا اسحق رحمه الله يقول كنت في جنب الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلى كقطرة في البحر وسمعت الشيخ أبا الحسن الباهلي قال كنت انا في جنب الشيخ الأشعرى كقطرة في جنب البحر .
Among them is Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him. The sheikh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Shu'ayri in Bistam informed me, saying: My maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad al-Sahlaki narrated to me, saying: One of the people of knowledge and Sufism narrated to me from the judge Abu Bakr ibn al-Baqillani, may Allah have mercy on him, who said: I, the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, and the master Ibn Furak, may Allah have mercy on them, were together in the lesson of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, the student of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The judge Abu Bakr said: Sheikh al-Bahili would teach us once every Friday, and he was behind a veil for us, lowering the curtain between us and him so that we would not see him. He said: And due to the intensity of his occupation with Allah Almighty, he was like one infatuated or insane; he did not know the extent of our lesson until we reminded him of that. He said: And we asked about the reason for the veil and the sending down of the curtain between him and these three, as he screened himself from everyone. He answered: You see the common people, who are people of heedlessness, so you see me with the eye with which you see them. He said: And there was also a young servant girl who served him, and her state was also like the state of others with him regarding the veil and the lowering of the curtain. Abu al-Muzaffar said: And I heard my grandfather say: I heard Sufyan the Sufi theologian, may Allah have mercy on him, say: I heard Ahmad al-Farsani, may Allah have mercy on him, say: I heard the master Abu Ishaq, may Allah have mercy on him, say: I was, in relation to Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, like a drop in the sea. And I heard Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili say: I was, in relation to Sheikh al-Ash'ari, like a drop in the sea.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, another student of al-Ash'ari. It consists of a chain of narration from Ibn Asakir back to al-Baqillani, who tells an anecdote about al-Bahili's astonishing spiritual state. Al-Baqillani narrates that three great scholars—himself, Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, and Ibn Furak—attended al-Bahili's lessons. Al-Bahili would teach only once a week and would sit behind a curtain so that his students could not see him. The reason was his intense absorption in Allah, to the point of appearing infatuated or mad; he would even forget the extent of the lesson until reminded. When asked why he veiled himself, al-Bahili replied that the students, being like the common people who are heedless, would see him with the same eye they see others—implying that his state was beyond their perception. Even the maidservant who served him was subject to the same veiling. The anecdote concludes with a series of humility statements: Abu Ishaq considered himself a drop in the sea compared to al-Bahili, and al-Bahili considered himself a drop in the sea compared to al-Ash'ari. This illustrates the high spiritual rank of these early Ash'ari scholars and their deep devotion.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الباهلي
أبو بكر بن الباقلاني
أبو اسحق الاسفرايني
ابن فورك
حجاب
اشتعاله بالله
قطرة في البحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The veil (hijab) indicates secrecy or hiding knowledge from students.
Correction: The veil was a reflection of his overwhelming absorption in Allah; he was not hiding knowledge but protecting his students from distraction.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Bahili teach behind a curtain according to al-Baqillani's account?
A) He was shy of his students
B) He was too busy with worldly affairs
C) His intense occupation with Allah made him unaware of their presence
D) He wanted to charge extra fees

Key Takeaway
The intensity of al-Bahili's devotion caused him to veil himself from his students, and this story teaches us the immense spiritual station of the early Ash'ari scholars and their deep humility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bahili was so absorbed in Allah that he lost awareness of his students. When was the last time you were so absorbed in worship or remembrance that you lost track of time? Are you content with your current level of devotion, or are you satisfied with being heedless like the common people? What specific change will you make tomorrow to deepen your absorption in Allah?
?
Al-Bahili veiled himself because his students saw him 'with the eye with which they see the heedless.' Do you judge righteous people by worldly standards? Have you ever dismissed someone sincerely devoted to Allah because they didn't fit your image of piety? How can you train your heart to see the true state of believers?
?
Three of the greatest scholars of their time considered themselves like a drop in the sea compared to their teacher. Do you have genuine humility before those who have taught you? Or do you feel superior? Before you sleep tonight, think of one teacher or scholar and write down a concrete way you can acknowledge your smallness next to them.

Bandar ibn al-Husayn: A Sufi Theologian’s Defense of the Shaykhs
Biography of Bandar ibn al-Husayn including his debate with Ibn Khafif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bandar ibn al-Husayn's role as a Sufi theologian who defended the sayings of the early shaykhs against the critiques of Muhammad ibn Khafif.

Core Idea
Learning and defending tradition: Bandar upheld the shaykhs' teachings against Ibn Khafif.

This passage from Ibn Asakir presents the biography of Bandar ibn al-Husayn, a learned Sufi and theologian. It highlights his expertise in principles (usul) and his famous eloquence in the science of reality. The key event is his debate with Muhammad ibn Khafif over the issue of faith, where Bandar defended the traditional positions of the shaykhs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of narration
Notice that Ibn Asakir transmits this biography through al-Sulami's 'History of the Sufis'. Identify the narrators and understand the scholarly chain.
This chain shows the reliability and transmission of the account.
2
Understand Bandar's description
Bandar is described as learned in usul and eloquent in ilm al-haqiqa. Note how these attributes place him among both theologians and Sufis.
Ilm al-haqiqa refers to spiritual realities.
3
Analyze the debate with Ibn Khafif
The passage mentions that Bandar refuted Muhammad ibn Khafif's refutation of the shaykhs' sayings on the issue of iman (faith). This shows Bandar's commitment to orthodox Sufi positions.
4
Reflect on the death note
The death year and the mention of Abu Zur'a washing Bandar indicate his status and provide historical context.
353 AH is around 964 CE.

ومنهم أبو الحسين بندار بن الحسين الشيرازي الصوفي خادم أبي الحسن رحمهما الله أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن اسماعيل بن عبد الغافر في كتابه، قال انا أبو بكر محمد بن يحيى بن إبراهيم المزكي قال انا أبو عبد عبد الرحمن محمد بن الحسين السلمي في كتاب تاريخ الصوفية قال : بندار بن الحسين بن محمد بن المهلب أبو الحسين من أهل شيراز سكن أرجان وكان عالما بالأصول له اللسان المشهور في علم الحقيقة كان الشبلي يكرمه ويقدمه وبينه وبين محمد بن خفيف مفاوضات في مسائل رد على محمد بن خفيف في مسألة الايمان وغيرها حين رد محمد بن خفیف علی اقاويل المشايخ فصوب بندار اقاويل المشايخ ورد عليه ما رد عليهم قال أبو عبد الرحمن السلمى سمعت عبدالواحد بن محمد يقول توفى بندار سنة ثلاث وخمسين وثلاثمائة وغسله أبو زرعة ،الطبرى، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو السعود أحمد بن علی بن محمد بن المجلى الواعظ ببغداد قال انا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب الحافظ قال أخبرنا محمد بن أبي على الأصبهاني قال : سمعت أبا بكر النسوى يقول سمعت بندار بن الحسين يقول من مشى في الظلمة إلى ذي النعم إجلسه علي بساط الكرم ومن قطع لسانه بشفرة السكوت بنى له بيت فى الملكوت ومن واصل اهل الجهالة ألبس ثوب البطالة، ومن أكثر تذكر الله تعالى شغله عن ذكر الناس ومن
Among them is Abu al-Husayn Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi al-Sufi, the servant of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on them. Sheikh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Ismail ibn Abd al-Ghafir informed us in his book, saying: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Yahya ibn Ibrahim al-Muzakki narrated to us, saying: Abu Abd al-Rahman Muhammad ibn al-Husayn al-Sulami narrated to us in his book The History of the Sufis, said: Bandar ibn al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn al-Muhallab, Abu al-Husayn, from the people of Shiraz, resided in Arjan. He was learned in the principles (usul), had the famous tongue in the science of reality (ilm al-haqiqa). Al-Shibli used to honor him and give him precedence. Between him and Muhammad ibn Khafif there were discussions on issues. He refuted Muhammad ibn Khafif on the issue of faith (iman) and others, when Muhammad ibn Khafif had refuted the sayings of the shaykhs. So Bandar upheld the sayings of the shaykhs and refuted him concerning what he had refuted against them. Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami said: I heard Abd al-Wahid ibn Muhammad say: Bandar died in the year three hundred and fifty-three (353 AH), and Abu Zur'a [al-Tabari] washed him. Whoever cuts his tongue with the blade of silence, He builds for him a house in the kingdom (malakut). Whoever associates with the people of ignorance, He clothes him in the garment of laziness. Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Abu al-Husayn Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi, a Sufi and mutakallim who served Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (likely). Ibn Asakir provides a chain through al-Sulami's History of the Sufis. Bandar is described as learned in usul (theological principles) and eloquent in ilm al-haqiqa (the science of spiritual reality). He was honored by the famous Sufi al-Shibli. Importantly, he engaged in theological debates with Muhammad ibn Khafif, a well-known Sufi. When Ibn Khafif refuted the sayings of earlier shaykhs (mashaikh) on the issue of iman (faith), Bandar defended the shaykhs and refuted Ibn Khafif's refutation. This shows Bandar's role in preserving orthodox Sufi and theological positions. The passage then gives his death date (353 AH) and that he was washed by Abu Zur'a al-Tabari. Lastly, an additional chain provides a set of maxims or aphorisms (hikam) from Bandar. These describe spiritual states: walking in darkness to Allah brings generosity; silence builds a house in the malakut (the spiritual realm); associating with ignorant leads to laziness; and remembering Allah distracts from people. The text cuts off mid-sentence at 'and from...' (ومن), likely continuing with more benefits of dhikr. This biography highlights the integration of theology and Sufism among early Ash'ari circles.

Key Takeaway
Bandar ibn al-Husayn was both a learned theologian and a Sufi who defended the teachings of the early shaykhs against theological upset.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear teachings that challenge your cherished beliefs—like Bandar heard from Ibn Khafif—do you instinctively reject them or do you examine them against the foundation of knowledge you have been taught? Bandar did not reject Ibn Khafif outright; he engaged and used his knowledge to defend the truth. Are you willing to study deeply enough to defend your faith when it is questioned, or do you remain silent out of ignorance?
?
Bandar was honored by al-Shibli and others, yet he did not hesitate to refute a respected scholar like Ibn Khafif. Who in your life do you look up to so much that you would be afraid to correct them if they said something wrong about Islam? How would you handle that situation in a way that upholds the truth while maintaining respect?
?
The passage emphasizes that Bandar was learned in usul (principles) and 'ilm al-haqiqa (spiritual reality). Are you investing time to learn the principles of your faith so that you can distinguish truth from error, or are you content with following the crowd? What step will you take today to deepen your understanding of Islamic theology?

The Spiritual Aphorisms of Bandar ibn al-Husayn
Aphorisms from Bandar ibn al-Husayn about spiritual states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual sayings attributed to Bandar ibn al-Husayn, which highlight the rewards of seeking Allah, silence, and remembrance.

Core Idea
Silence and seeking Allah lead to spiritual houses in the malakut.

The second part of the biography presents a set of aphorisms from Bandar ibn al-Husayn. These pithy sayings encapsulate spiritual principles: walking in darkness to Allah brings generosity, cutting the tongue with silence builds a house in the spiritual kingdom, and remembering Allah distracts from people. They reflect the integration of Sufi spirituality with theological learning.

Guided Path
1
Examine the chain of transmission
Note that this saying comes through a separate chain from al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. This gives it a different authority.
The chain is: Ibn Asakir from Abu al-Sa'ud from al-Khatib from Muhammad ibn Abi Ali from Abu Bakr al-Nasawi from Bandar.
2
Analyze each aphorism
The four sayings are conditional statements linking a human action to a divine response or consequence. Identify the condition and the result in each.
The first three are positive actions (walking in darkness, cutting tongue, associating with ignorant) and their outcomes.
3
Reflect on the spiritual meanings
Consider the symbolism: 'darkness to the One of blessings' suggests sincere effort, 'blade of silence' implies self-discipline, and 'house in the malakut' indicates spiritual rank.
4
Understand the incomplete last saying
The passage cuts off on 'and from...' but the complete saying likely contrasts remembrance with something else.
The text is cut due to page boundary.

Humility of Scholars: A Drop in the Sea
Anecdote comparing al-Bahili's status relative to al-Ash'ari to a drop in the sea.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound humility of early Ash'ari scholars and how they recognized the vast disparity in knowledge between teachers and students, as illustrated by the metaphor of a drop in the sea.

Core Idea
True scholars measure themselves humbly, like a drop in the sea.

This lesson presents a brief anecdote from the biographical tradition that reveals the character of early Ash'ari scholars. Through a chain of narrators, we see how Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili expressed their own insignificance compared to their teachers. The metaphor of a drop in the sea is a powerful reminder of the humility that comes with true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the chain of narration
Identify each narrator in the isnad and note how the story is transmitted.
The chain shows careful preservation of the anecdote.
2
Understand the metaphor
The phrase 'drop in the sea' expresses extreme humility. Reflect on what it means to compare one's knowledge to a drop.
3
See the repetition
Notice that al-Bahili uses the same metaphor about al-Ash'ari, showing the depth of his respect.
Repetition emphasizes the point.
4
Appreciate the ethical lesson
True knowledge leads to humility, not arrogance. Consider how this applies to your own learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو المظفر وسمعت جدى يقول سمعت سفيان المتكلم الصوفى رحمه الله يقول سمعت أحمد الفرسانى رحمه الله يقول سمعت الأستاذ أبا اسحق رحمه الله يقول كنت في جنب الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلى كقطرة في البحر وسمعت الشيخ أبا الحسن الباهلي قال كنت انا في جنب الشيخ الأشعرى كقطرة في جنب البحر .
Abu al-Muzaffar said: I heard my grandfather say: I heard Sufyan al-Mutakallim al-Sufi, may God have mercy on him, say: I heard Ahmad al-Farsani, may God have mercy on him, say: I heard the master Abu Ishaq, may God have mercy on him, say: I was next to the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili like a drop in the sea. And I heard the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili say: I was next to the Shaykh al-Ash'ari like a drop in the sea.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a chain of transmission (isnad) containing an anecdote about the humility of scholars. Each narrator conveys a comparison illustrating the vast disparity in knowledge between a student and his teacher. Abu Ishaq describes himself relative to al-Bahili as 'a drop in the sea,' meaning his knowledge was insignificant compared to al-Bahili's. Then al-Bahili himself says the same regarding his own relation to al-Ash'ari, emphasizing al-Ash'ari's supreme stature in learning. The repetition underscores the deep respect and humility characteristic of these early scholars.

Key Terms
قطرة في البحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This anecdote is a literal measurement of knowledge quantity.
Correction: The phrase 'drop in the sea' is a metaphor for humility, not a precise measurement.

Checkpoint
What comparison is used to describe the difference in knowledge between al-Bahili and al-Ash'ari?
A) A mountain and a pebble
B) A drop in the sea
C) A star and the moon
D) A teacher and a student

Key Takeaway
The humility of scholars is a sign of true knowledge, as exemplified by al-Bahili's self-comparison to a drop in the sea relative to al-Ash'ari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt like a 'drop in the sea' next to someone with more knowledge? How did that feeling affect your pursuit of learning, and what will you do this week to intentionally place yourself in a position of learning from those more knowledgeable?
?
Do you ever find yourself feeling proud of your own knowledge, comparing yourself favorably to others? How can the example of al-Bahili and al-Ash'ari reshape your attitude? Write one concrete action you will take to humble yourself before a teacher or peer.
?
Imagine attending a gathering of scholars where you are the least knowledgeable person present. Would you feel humiliated or honored? How can you cultivate a love for being the smallest drop in the sea of knowledge? Commit to one step you will take this week to seek such environments.

Biography of Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi
Biographical entry for Bandar including his lineage, teachers, and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life, scholarly contributions, and key debates of the 4th-century Sufi scholar Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi.

Core Idea
Bandar al-Shirazi defended the early shaykhs against criticisms in debates on faith.

This lesson introduces a brief yet rich biographical entry for Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi, a prominent Sufi scholar of the 4th century AH. The passage provides his lineage, his expertise in usul, and his known eloquence in the science of reality. It also highlights his important debates with Muhammad ibn Khafif regarding the sayings of the early shaykhs.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical entry carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the chain of transmission and the key details about Bandar's life.
Pay attention to the names of the narrators and the date of death.
2
Identify the key facts
Identify Bandar's full name, place of origin, residence, and the year of his death. Notice his role as a servant of Abu al-Hasan.
These facts form the basic outline of his biography.
3
Understand his scholarly standing
Notice that he was described as 'learned in usul' and having 'a famous tongue in the science of reality'. These are high praises in the Sufi tradition.
Usul refers to the foundations of religion; haqiqa refers to spiritual reality.
4
Examine his relationships and debates
See how al-Shibli honored him and how he debated Muhammad ibn Khafif. Bandar defended the sayings of the early shaykhs against Ibn Khafif's criticisms.
This shows his importance in preserving the teachings of the early Sufis.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this biography is included: it shows a model Sufi scholar who combined knowledge and spiritual authority.
The entry also provides historical data about the transmission of Sufi teachings.

ومنهم أبو الحسين بندار بن الحسين الشيرازي الصوفي خادم أبي الحسن رحمهما الله أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن اسماعيل بن عبد الغافر في كتابه، قال انا أبو بكر محمد بن يحيى بن إبراهيم المزكي قال انا أبو عبد عبد الرحمن محمد بن الحسين السلمي في كتاب تاريخ الصوفية قال : بندار بن الحسين بن محمد بن المهلب أبو الحسين من أهل شيراز سكن أرجان وكان عالما بالأصول له اللسان المشهور في علم الحقيقة كان الشبلي يكرمه ويقدمه وبينه وبين محمد بن خفيف مفاوضات في مسائل رد على محمد بن خفيف في مسألة الايمان وغيرها حين رد محمد بن خفيف علی اقاويل المشايخ فصوب بندار اقاويل المشايخ ورد عليه ما رد عليهم قال أبو عبد الرحمن السلمى سمعت عبدالواحد بن محمد يقول توفى بندار سنة ثلاث وخمسين وثلاثمائة وغسله أبو زرعة الطبرى.
And among them is Abu al-Husayn Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi al-Sufi, the servant of Abu al-Hasan, may God have mercy on them both. The Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Ismail ibn Abd al-Ghafir informed us in his book, saying: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Yahya ibn Ibrahim al-Muzakki told us, saying: Abu Abd Abd al-Rahman Muhammad ibn al-Husayn al-Sulami told us in the book 'History of the Sufis', saying: Bandar ibn al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn al-Muhallab Abu al-Husayn, from the people of Shiraz, resided in Arjan. He was learned in the fundamentals (usul), possessing a famous tongue in the science of reality. Al-Shibli honored him and gave him precedence. Between him and Muhammad ibn Khafif there were discussions on issues. He refuted Muhammad ibn Khafif on the issue of faith (iman) and others when Muhammad ibn Khafif refuted the sayings of the shaykhs, so Bandar affirmed the sayings of the shaykhs and refuted him regarding what he had refuted against them. Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami said: I heard Abd al-Wahid ibn Muhammad say: Bandar died in the year 353 AH, and Abu Zur'a al-Tabari washed him.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Bandar ibn al-Husayn. It provides his full name, origin (Shiraz), residence (Arjan), and his role as a servant of Abu al-Hasan (likely al-Ash'ari). It highlights his expertise in usul (foundations of religion) and his renowned eloquence in the science of reality (haqiqa). The entry mentions his close relationship with al-Shibli and his debates with Muhammad ibn Khafif on matters of faith and the sayings of the early shaykhs. Bandar defended the shaykhs against Ibn Khafif's criticisms. The death date is given as 353 AH, and his ritual washing was performed by Abu Zur'a al-Tabari.

Misconception: Bandar was a companion of the Prophet Muhammad.
Correction: Bandar lived in the 4th century AH, over 300 years after the Prophet, and was a later Sufi scholar.

Key Takeaway
Bandar ibn al-Husayn was a 4th-century Sufi scholar known for his expertise in usul and his defense of the early shaykhs' teachings against criticism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Shibli honored Bandar and gave him precedence. Are there people in your community whom you truly honor by giving them precedence in your time and attention, or do you merely respect them from a distance? Whom could you honor more actively starting today?
?
Bandar debated Ibn Khafif on the issue of faith (iman). Have you ever had to defend a core Islamic belief against someone who seemed more knowledgeable? Did you prepare by studying the usul (foundations) like Bandar did, or did you rely on emotion? What step will you take this week to strengthen your understanding of a fundamental belief you hold?

The Mirror as a Spiritual Gauge
Story of Bandar's spiritual transformation under al-Shibli using a mirror as a gauge of attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shibli used a mirror metaphorically to guide Bandar toward complete detachment from wealth and social status, and what this reveals about Sufi concepts of purification.

Core Idea
The mirror reflects the heart's attachments: first wealth, then status.

This lesson presents a remarkable story from the early Sufi tradition, in which the great master al-Shibli uses a mirror not for vanity but as a tool of spiritual insight. Through repeated interactions, he guides his disciple Bandar to relinquish first his entire fortune, then his desire for social standing. The story illustrates the Sufi path of tazkiyah (purification) and the principle that true devotion leaves no room for worldly attachment.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the repetition and the progression of the mirror's 'statements'.
The story has two versions; observe how they complement each other.
2
Identify the role of the mirror
What does the mirror represent? In the first version, it seems to speak; in the second, it is explained as al-Shibli's heart.
The mirror is not magical—it symbolizes spiritual insight.
3
Understand the meaning of 'cause' (sabab)
'Sabab' here means any attachment or reason that still ties Bandar to his wealth. The mirror 'says' there is still a cause until all wealth is given.
Detachment means removing all causes that keep the heart from God.
4
See the progression from wealth to status
After wealth, al-Shibli tells Bandar to give up status. Bandar then goes begging, showing humility.
True tawakkul involves abandoning even the desire for respect.
5
Reflect on the spiritual lesson
Consider what attachments you might have that are 'causes' keeping you from complete reliance on Allah.
This story is a practical example of zuhd (asceticism).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الحسن ابن اسماعيل الفارسي في كتابه قال انا أبو بكر بن زكريا بن أبى اسحق قال انا محمد بن الحسين الصوفى قال سمعت عبد الواحد بن محمد يقول سمعت بندارا يقول اول مادخلت على الشبلى وكان معي جهاز نحو اربعين الف دينار فنظر الشبلى فى المرآة فقال يا أبا الحسن المرآة تقول ان ثم سبب، فقلت ضدقت المرآة فحملت اليه ست بدر ثم نظر بعد ذلك في المرآة فقال المرآة تقول ان ثم سبب فقلت صدق المرآة فحملت اليه ثلاث بدر فكلما اجتمع عندى من جهازي شيء كان ينظر في المرآة ويقول المرآة تقول ان ثم سبب حملت جميع مالى اليه فنظر في المرآة وقال المرآة تقول ليس ثم سبب قلت صدق المرآة، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو نصر عبد الرحيم بن عبدالكريم بن هوازن في كتابه قال سمعت أبي الأستاذ أبا القسم يقول كان الأستاذ أبو بكر بن فورك رحمه الله يحكى عن بندار بن الحسين الشيرازي انه كان من أصحاب الشبلى وكان ابوه جهزه إلى بغداد للتجارة فوقع الى مجلس الشبلي فأثر فيه كلامه فأمره الشبلى بالخروج عن المال فكان كلما حضر الشبلى نظر الشبلى فى مرآة عنده وكان يقول المرآة تقول قد بقى شيء وكانت المرآة على الحقيقة قلبه فكان بندار يقول صدقت المرآة وكان الشبلي يكثر النظر في المرآة فسئل عن ذلك فقال بينى وبين الله عهد ان ملت عنه عاقبني، فأنا أنظر فى كل ساعة فى المرآة هل اسود وجهى فلما لم يبق لبندار شيء قال الشبلى المرآة تقول لم يبق شيء فقال صدقت المرآة، فقال الشبلي فاخرج الآن من الجاه فجعل يدور على معارفة يكدى فكان بعضهم حنى
The Shaykh Abu al-Hasan ibn Ismail al-Farsi informed us in his book, saying: Abu Bakr ibn Zakariyya ibn Abi Ishaq told us, saying: Muhammad ibn al-Husayn al-Sufi told us, saying: I heard Abd al-Wahid ibn Muhammad say: I heard Bandar say: The first time I entered upon al-Shibli, and with me was wealth of about forty thousand dinars. Al-Shibli looked into the mirror and said: O Abu al-Hasan, the mirror says there is still a cause. So I said: The mirror spoke truth, and I brought him six bdrs (a unit of weight). Then he looked again into the mirror and said: The mirror says there is still a cause. I said: The mirror spoke truth, and I brought him three bdrs. Every time some of my wealth accrued, he would look into the mirror and say: The mirror says there is still a cause, until I brought all my wealth to him. Then he looked into the mirror and said: The mirror says there is no cause. I said: The mirror spoke truth. And the Shaykh Abu Nasr Abd al-Rahim ibn Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin informed us in his book, saying: I heard my father, the master Abu al-Qasim, say: The master Abu Bakr ibn Furak, may God have mercy on him, used to relate from Bandar ibn al-Husayn al-Shirazi that he was among the companions of al-Shibli. His father had equipped him to go to Baghdad for trade, and he happened upon al-Shibli's gathering. His speech affected him, so al-Shibli ordered him to abandon his wealth. Whenever al-Shibli came, he would look into a mirror he had and say: The mirror says something remains. In reality, the mirror was his heart. Bandar would say: The mirror spoke truth. Al-Shibli frequently looked into the mirror, and when asked about it, he said: There is a covenant between me and God that if I deviate from Him, He will punish me, so I look every hour into the mirror to see if my face has turned black. When nothing remained with Bandar, al-Shibli said: The mirror says nothing remains. He said: The mirror spoke truth. Then al-Shibli said: Now come out of (the desire for) status. So he began to go around to his acquaintances, begging... and some of them... (text breaks off).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage contains two versions of a story about Bandar's spiritual transformation under al-Shibli. The first version, from Ibn Isma'il al-Farsi, describes how Bandar brought 40,000 dinars to al-Shibli. Al-Shibli repeatedly looks into a mirror and says 'the mirror says there is still a cause (sabab),' meaning something still attaches Bandar to his wealth. Each time Bandar brings more money until all is given away. The mirror then declares 'no cause.' The second version, from Ibn Hawazin, explains that the mirror symbolizes al-Shibli's heart, which reflects his spiritual state. Al-Shibli used the mirror to check his own sincerity, and through it he discerned Bandar's lingering attachments. After Bandar gave all his wealth, al-Shibli told him to also give up status, leading him to beg from acquaintances. The story illustrates the Sufi concept of detachment from both wealth and social standing.

Key Terms
مرآة
سبب
جهاز
بدر
جاه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mirror is a magical object that literally speaks.
Correction: The mirror is a metaphor; it represents al-Shibli's spiritual insight or his heart, which discerns the state of Bandar's attachment.

Checkpoint
What did the mirror 'say' when Bandar still had wealth?
A) There is no cause
B) There is still a cause
C) You are truthful
D) Give more

Key Takeaway
The story demonstrates that true spiritual advancement requires detachment not only from wealth but also from the desire for status and recognition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the mirror of your heart could speak, what 'cause' (sabab) would it say still attaches you to this world? Are you ready to give it up today, not just in word but in action?
?
Al-Shibli constantly checked his heart for deviation from God. When did you last sincerely examine your intentions in a moment of solitude, and what did you find? Will you make that a daily habit starting tonight?
?
Bandar had to abandon both wealth and social standing. Which of these two weighs heavier on your heart? What is the first concrete step you will take this week to loosen its grip?
